<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: 88%">Range of <i>H. s. sapiens</i> (red and pink)</td>

<p>In <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropology</a>, <b>anatomically modern humans</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> (<b>AMH</b>) or <b>anatomically modern <i>Homo sapiens</i></b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> (<b>AMHS</b>) are the members of the <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> with an <a href="/wiki/Human_physical_appearance" title="Human physical appearance">appearance</a> consistent with the <a href="/wiki/Human_variability" title="Human variability">range</a> of <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypes</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>.</p>
<p>Anatomically modern humans (<b>Homo sapiens sapiens</b>) evolved from <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">archaic humans</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a>, at least 300,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The emergence of anatomically modern humans marks the dawn of the species <i>Homo sapiens</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> i.e. the species to which all humans alive today belong. Recent DNA analysis comparing the genome of a Stone Age <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khoi-San" title="Khoi-San">Khoi-San</a> skeleton with other modern African populations suggest that the Koi-San people diverged from the West African <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka people</a> approximately 356,000 years ago, indicating the origin of modern humans is older than that date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The oldest <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> remains of anatomically modern humans are the <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud" title="Jebel Irhoud">Jebel Irhoud</a> fossils from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> which are approximately 300,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Other important early modern finds include the <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a> found in modern-day <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, which date to 195,000 years ago and include two partial skulls as well as arm, leg, foot and pelvis bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tignor2014_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tignor2014-9">[9]</a></sup> Other fossils include the proposed <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_idaltu" title="Homo sapiens idaltu">Homo sapiens idaltu</a></i> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herto_Bouri" title="Herto Bouri">Herto</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> that are almost <a href="/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic" title="Lower Paleolithic">160,000 years old</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White03_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-White03-10">[10]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skhul_and_Qafzeh_hominids" title="Skhul and Qafzeh hominids">Skhul hominids</a> from <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, which are 90,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The oldest human remains from which an entire genome has been extracted belongs to <a href="/wiki/Ust%27-Ishim_man" title="Ust'-Ishim man">Ust'-Ishim man</a>, who lived about 45,000 years ago in Western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">Behavioral modernity</a>—a suite of changes in <i>Homo sapiens</i> behavior and <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> including abstract thinking, deep planning, symbolic behavior (e.g. art, ornamentation and music), exploitation of large game and blade technology—is evident from around 40,000–50,000 years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_1995_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_1995-13">[13]</a></sup> and may have emerged abruptly then or may have arisen through gradual steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McBrearty_Brooks_2000_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-McBrearty_Brooks_2000-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henshilwood_Marean_2003_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henshilwood_Marean_2003-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marean_et_al_2007_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marean_et_al_2007-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell_et_al_2009_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powell_et_al_2009-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Premo_Kuhn_2010_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Premo_Kuhn_2010-18">[18]</a></sup> However, it has been argued that <i>Homo sapiens</i> have been fully capable of modern behavior from the time they first evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature_and_anatomy">Nomenclature and anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature and anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">
<div id="Title" style="background-color:#77bb77; font-weight:bold; padding:0.2em; text-align:center; margin:0.6em 0 1.2em 0"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_evolution" title="Timeline of human evolution">Human timeline</a></div>
<div id="Contains-Timeline-Scale-Notes">
<div style="text-align:right; position:relative; top:-1em;"><small><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline"><span style="color:#002bb8;" title="View this timeline in its own page">view</span></a> • <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_timeline" title="Template talk:Human timeline"><span style="color:#002bb8;" title="Discussion about this template">discuss</span></a> • <span class="noprint plainlinksneverexpand" style="color:#002bb8;" title="You can edit this timeline. <div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years ago</a>.<br/>
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">Life timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_anatomy" title="Human anatomy">Human anatomy</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial name</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">human population</a> is <i>Homo sapiens</i>. The species is usually taken to have emerged from a predecessor within the <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> genus around 200,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_build">General build</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: General build">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Generally, modern humans are more lightly built than <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">archaic humans</a> from which they have evolved. Humans are a highly variable species; modern humans can show remarkably robust traits, and early modern humans even more so. Despite this, modern humans differ from archaic people (the <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovans</a>) in a range of anatomical details.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Braincase_anatomy">Braincase anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Braincase anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="923" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg/300px-Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg/450px-Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg/600px-Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anatomical comparison of skulls of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> (left) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_neanderthalensis" title="Homo neanderthalensis">Homo neanderthalensis</a></i> (right)<br/>
(in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Cleveland Museum of Natural History">Cleveland Museum of Natural History</a>)<br/>
Features compared are the <a href="/wiki/Neurocranium" title="Neurocranium">braincase</a> shape, <a href="/wiki/Forehead" title="Forehead">forehead</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Browridge" title="Browridge">browridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasal_bone" title="Nasal bone">nasal bone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasal_bone" title="Nasal bone">projection</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheek_bone" title="Cheek bone">cheek bone angulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chin" title="Chin">chin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Occipital_bone" title="Occipital bone">occipital contour</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cranium lacks a pronounced <a href="/wiki/Occipital_bun" title="Occipital bun">occipital bun</a> in the neck, a bulge that anchored considerable neck muscles in Neanderthals. Modern humans, even the earlier ones, generally have a larger fore-brain than the archaic people, so that the brain sits above rather than behind the eyes. This will usually (though not always) give a higher forehead, and reduced <a href="/wiki/Supraorbital_ridge" title="Supraorbital ridge">brow ridge</a>. Early modern people and some living people do however have quite pronounced brow ridges, but they differ from those of archaic forms by having both a <a href="/wiki/Supraorbital_foramen" title="Supraorbital foramen">supraorbital foramen</a> or notch, forming a groove through the ridge above each eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This splits the ridge into a central part and two distal parts. In current humans, often only the central section of the ridge is preserved (if it is preserved at all). This contrasts with archaic humans, where the brow ridge is pronounced and unbroken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern humans commonly have a steep, even vertical <a href="/wiki/Forehead" title="Forehead">forehead</a> whereas their predecessors had foreheads that sloped strongly backwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Morris" title="Desmond Morris">Desmond Morris</a>, the vertical forehead in humans plays an important role in human communication through <a href="/wiki/Eyebrow" title="Eyebrow">eyebrow</a> movements and forehead skin wrinkling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jaw_anatomy">Jaw anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Jaw anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Compared to archaic people, anatomically modern humans have smaller, differently shaped teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Townsend_G.2C_Richards_L.2C_Hughes_T_2003_350.E2.80.935_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Townsend_G.2C_Richards_L.2C_Hughes_T_2003_350.E2.80.935-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This results in a smaller, more receded dentary, making the rest of the jaw-line stand out, giving an often quite prominent chin. The central part of the mandible forming the chin carries a triangularly shaped area forming the apex of the chin called the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_trigon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mental trigon (page does not exist)">mental trigon</a>, not found in archaic humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Particularly in living population, the use of fire and tools require fewer jaw muscles, giving slender, more gracile jaws. Compared to archaic people, modern humans have smaller, lower faces.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Body_skeleton">Body skeleton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Body skeleton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The body skeletons of even the earliest and most robustly built modern humans were less robust than those of Neanderthals (and from what little we know from Denisovans), having essentially modern proportions. Particularly regarding the long bones of the limbs, the distal bones (the <a href="/wiki/Radius_(bone)" title="Radius (bone)">radius</a>/<a href="/wiki/Ulna" title="Ulna">ulna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a>/<a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">fibula</a>) are nearly the same size or slightly shorter than the proximal bones (the <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a>). In ancient people, particularly Neanderthals, the distal bones were shorter, usually thought to be an adaptation to cold climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The same adaptation can be found in some modern people living in the polar regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Archaic <i>Homo sapiens</i> had robust <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletons" title="Skeletons">skeletons</a> indicating that they lived a physically demanding life; this can mean that anatomically modern humans, with their <a href="/wiki/Gracility" title="Gracility">gracile</a> frames, had become dependent on <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> rather than on raw <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical power</a> to meet the challenges of their environment.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_physical_appearance" title="Human physical appearance">Human physical appearance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_variability" title="Human variability">Human variability</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homo_genus"><i>Homo</i> genus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Homo genus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">Archaic humans</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meganthropus" title="Meganthropus">Meganthropus</a></div>
<p>The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> emerges from <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">australopithecine</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominin" title="Hominin">hominins</a> some time after 3 million years ago. With the arrival of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> in the fossil record ca. <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1.8–1.3">1.8 to 1.3</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, cranial capacity had doubled to 850 cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It is believed that <i>Homo erectus</i> and <i>Homo ergaster</i> were the first to use fire and complex tools. Modern humans evolved from <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">Homo heidelbergensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">Homo rhodesiensis</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_antecessor" title="Homo antecessor">Homo antecessor</a></i> and, some 100,000 to 50,000 years ago, took the place of local populations of <i>Homo erectus</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_denisova" title="Homo denisova">Homo denisova</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">Homo floresiensis</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_neanderthalensis" title="Homo neanderthalensis">Homo neanderthalensis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encylopediahumanevolution_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-encylopediahumanevolution-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolutionthe1st4billionyears_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-evolutionthe1st4billionyears-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_evolution" title="Timeline of human evolution">Timeline of human evolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">Hominini</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominina" title="Hominina">Hominina</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_Evolution" title="Human Evolution">Human Evolution</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_origin_models">Major origin models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Major origin models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin of modern humans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a></div>
<table style="float:right; clear:right;">
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="color:#black; background:#ddd; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Major origin models</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:32px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-human-migrations.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="889" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Map-of-human-migrations.jpg/120px-Map-of-human-migrations.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Map-of-human-migrations.jpg/180px-Map-of-human-migrations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Map-of-human-migrations.jpg/240px-Map-of-human-migrations.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin</a> model<br/>
<small>Africa start of the migration; Numbers represent 1000 yrs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_present" title="Before present">before present</a>. (North Pole at center)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></small></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>As it is usually presented, there are two major competing models on this subject—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin" title="Recent African origin">recent African origin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiregional_evolution" title="Multiregional evolution">multiregional evolution</a>. The debate concerns both the <i>relative</i> amount of replacement or interbreeding that occurred in areas outside of Africa, when waves of humans (or human ancestors) left it to colonize other areas, and the relative importance of more recent waves as opposed to more ancient ones.</p>
<table align="left">
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="color:#black; background:#ddd; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">mtDNA Tree</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1743" data-file-width="1084" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg/74px-MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg/112px-MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg/149px-MacaulayRichardsmtDNAtree.jpg 2x" width="74"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Human mtDNA tree<br/>
<small>("<a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve" title="Mitochondrial Eve">Mitochondrial Eve</a>" near top<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>)</small></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The mainstream scientific view, known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa_theory" title="Out of Africa theory">Out of Africa</a>" or "recent African origin" model, holds that all or nearly all modern human genetic diversity around the world can be traced back to the first anatomically modern humans to leave Africa. This model is supported by multiple and independent lines of evidence, such as the fossil record and genetics. The precise location where AMH first emerged is still unclear, but the consensus in 2014 indicates an origin within <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>. The most genetically distinct contemporary human populations are hunter-gatherers within southern Africa. In 2014 an mtDNA analysis on a skeleton over 2000 years old was reported. This individual predated the movement of genetically- and culturally-distinct pastoral peoples into this region, and thus had been out of touch with other Homo sapiens for an extended period. The analysis indicated a maternal line closely linked to that found in "Mitochondrial Eve". This supports archeological and osteological evidence indicating the presence, in southern Africa, of marine foragers with ancient maternal human mtDNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> This also supports views suggesting initial maritime dispersal of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, critics of this view are often bracketed together as holding a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiregional_hypothesis" title="Multiregional hypothesis">multiregional hypothesis</a>", which was being studied in the early 1980s into the 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Such critics argue that significant amounts of older non-African genetic lineages have survived in various parts of the world through inter-breeding with anatomically modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> According to versions of the multiregional model the various human populations around the world today will have surviving genetic material that goes back as far as early humans such as <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Human_evolutionary_genetics" title="Human evolutionary genetics">human evolutionary genetics</a> <a href="/wiki/Data_set" title="Data set">data set</a> (Jobling, Hurles and Tyler-Smith, 2004) favor the "Out of Africa" model. Analyses of modern Europeans suggest that no <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (direct maternal line) originating with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> has survived into modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The recent sequencing of the Neanderthal and <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovan</a> genomes <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">shows possible admixture</a>. A draft sequence publication by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthal_Genome_Project" title="Neanderthal Genome Project">Neanderthal Genome Project</a> in May 2010 indicates some form of hybridization between archaic humans and modern humans took place after modern humans emerged from Africa. An estimated 1 to 4 percent of the DNA in Europeans and Asians (i.e. French, Chinese and Papua probands) is non-modern, and shared with ancient Neanderthal <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and not with Sub-Saharan Africans (i.e., Yoruba and San probands),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a> have an additional 1–6% of Denisovan origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In practice, controversy is generally about specific periods and specific proposals for periods of such interbreeding, in addition to whether such interbreeding took place. The existence and importance of <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> out of Africa is generally accepted, while the possibility of isolated instances of inter-breeding between recent sub-Saharan arrivals and their less "modern" contemporaries at various stages of prehistory is not particularly controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2014)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Nonetheless, and according to recent <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic studies</a>, modern humans may have mated with "at least two groups" of <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">ancient humans</a>: Neanderthals and Denisovans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-01302012_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-01302012-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2013 study, the researchers suggested that the observed genetic affinities between archaic and modern human populations are mostly explained by common ancestral polymorphisms—and not admixture—followed by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a>, explaining that the differences in the observed genetic affinities among modern human populations are most-likely a result from different retention rates of these polymorphisms among the modern human populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowcompoly_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-lowcompoly-47">[47]</a></sup> However, they also stated that the study did not rule out archaic introgression to modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowcompoly_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-lowcompoly-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies published in March 2016 suggest that modern humans bred with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominin" title="Hominin">hominins</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a>, on multiple occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160317_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160317-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_humans">Early modern humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early modern humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Race_(biology)" title="Race (biology)">Race (biology)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">Species</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">Subspecies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">Systematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">Phylogenetics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">Cladistics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skhul.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="549" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Skhul.JPG/200px-Skhul.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Skhul.JPG/300px-Skhul.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Skhul.JPG/400px-Skhul.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skhul.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skhul_V" title="Skhul V">Skhul V</a> (c. 100,000 BC) exhibiting a mix of archaic and modern traits.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_finds">History of finds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History of finds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first early modern humans to be found in Europe were the <a href="/wiki/Cro-Magnon" title="Cro-Magnon">Cro-Magnon</a>. During the latter half of the 20th century, new finds from all over the world, like those quoted above and others, greatly expanded our knowledge of the origin and spread of modern humans. The term Cro-Magnon was sometimes used for all of these, but has now been replaced by the term <b>early modern humans</b> in literature, the term Cro-Magnon now used for finds similar to the original find.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brace_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brace-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fagan_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fagan-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robusticity_of_early_modern_humans">Robusticity of early modern humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Robusticity of early modern humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many of the early modern human finds, like those of <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_idaltu" title="Homo sapiens idaltu">Herto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skhul_remains" title="Skhul remains">Skhul</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pe%C8%99tera_cu_Oase" title="Peștera cu Oase">Peștera cu Oase</a> exhibit a mix of archaic and modern traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Skhul V, for example, has prominent brow ridges and a projecting face. However, the <a href="/wiki/Neurocranium" title="Neurocranium">brain case</a> is quite rounded and distinct from that of the Neanderthals and is similar to the brain case of modern humans. It is now known that modern humans north of Sahara and outside of Africa have some <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">archaic human admixture</a>, though whether the robust traits of some of the early modern humans like Skhul V reflects mixed ancestry or retention of older traits is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reich_et_al._54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reich_et_al.-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terms_and_classification">Terms and classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Terms and classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "early" when applied to modern humans is usually restricted to finds from the upper <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, ending about 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fagan_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fagan-51">[51]</a></sup> This coincides with the end of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">last ice age</a>, which also saw the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">end of the ice age megafauna</a>. At this point the human population of the world switched from a culture of big game hunting to smaller game and later, an initially tiny proportion, began to exploit wild grains and starchy plants which could be stored. This led to increased sedentism, increased local population density, and eventually, by at least 8,000 years ago, to the domestication of plants, as well as livestock species of animals, and then to mixed intensive agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin1989_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1989-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Updating_Martin_2008_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Updating_Martin_2008-57">[57]</a></sup> With higher population densities, better tools and fewer demands for brute strength, people all over the world became less robust, resulting in the comparatively more gracile population of today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Thus, anatomically modern humans can roughly be divided into two groups, the early (robust) and post-glacial (gracile) populations. The process leading to the development of smaller and more fine-boned humans seems to have begun at least 50,000–30,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are recognized subspecies, for example <i>H. s. sapiens</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_idaltu" title="Homo sapiens idaltu">H.s. idaltu</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="width:460px;">
<tr>
<th>Grouping</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">Subspecies</a></th>
<th><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Populations" title="Populations">Populations</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_human_behavior">Modern human behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Modern human behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hominization" title="Hominization">Hominization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">Behavioral modernity</a></div>
<p>There is considerable debate regarding whether the earliest anatomically modern humans behaved similarly to recent or existing humans. Modern human behaviors characteristic of recent humans includes a <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_language" title="Origin of language">language</a>, the capacity for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abstract_thought" title="Abstract thought">abstract thought</a> and the use of <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolism</a> to express cultural <a href="/wiki/Creativity" title="Creativity">creativity</a>. There are two opposing hypotheses regarding the origins of modern behavior. Some scholars argue that humans achieved anatomical modernity first, around 200,000 years ago. Later, around 50,000 years ago, humans then adopted modern behaviors. This hypothesis is based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">record of fossils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic substances</a> from periods before 50,000 years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">human artifacts</a> found after 50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Correspondingly, as stated by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Mellars" title="Paul Mellars">Paul Mellars</a>, the view distinguishes anatomically modern humans from <i>behaviorally</i> modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mellars_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-mellars-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The opposing view is that humans achieved anatomical and behavioral modernity simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> For example, most views argue that humans had evolved a lightly built skeleton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> During this transition to anatomical modernity, this could have occurred through increased human cooperation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Additionally, this could have occurred through the increased use of <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_technology" title="Prehistoric technology">technology</a>, traits characteristic of modern behaviour. Furthermore, there is evidence that the characteristic human brain development, especially the prefrontal cortex, due to "an exceptional acceleration of metabolome evolution ... paralleled by a drastic reduction in muscle strength. The observed rapid metabolic changes in brain and muscle, together with the unique human cognitive skills and low muscle performance, might reflect parallel mechanisms in human evolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sch%C3%B6ningen_spears" title="Schöningen spears">Schöningen spears</a> and their correlation of finds are evidence of complex technological skills already 300,000 years ago and are the first obvious proof for an active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_game_hunting" title="Big game hunting">(big game) hunt</a>. A successful hunt for quickly fleeing gregarious animals without sophisticated hunting strategies, a complex <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social structure</a> and developed forms of communication (language ability) is unlikely. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H._heidelbergensis" title="H. heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i> already had intellectual and cognitive skills like anticipatory planning, thinking and acting that so far has only been attributed to modern man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent archaeological data suggests that the essential elements of the kind of material culture typical of contemporary <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San</a> hunter-gatherers was already present at least 40,000 years ago, including digging sticks of similar materials used today, ostrich egg shell beads, bone arrow heads with individual maker's marks etched and embedded with red ochre, and poison applicators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> There is also a suggestion that "pressure flaking best explains the morphology of lithic artifacts recovered from the c. 75-ka Middle Stone Age levels at Blombos Cave, South Africa. The technique was used during the final shaping of Still Bay bifacial points made on heat‐treated silcrete."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Both pressure flaking and heat treatment of materials was previously thought to have occurred much later in prehistory, and both indicate a behaviourally modern sophistication in the use of natural materials. Further reports of research on caves sites along the southern African coast indicate that "the debate as to when cultural and cognitive characteristics typical of modern humans first appeared" may be coming to an end, as "advanced technologies with elaborate chains of production" which "often demand high-fidelity transmission and thus language" have been found at Pinnacle Point Site 5–6. These have been dated to approximately 71,000 years ago. The researchers suggest that their research "shows that microlithic technology originated early in South Africa, evolved over a vast time span (c. 11,000 years), and was typically coupled to complex heat treatment that persisted for nearly 100,000 years. Advanced technologies in Africa were early and enduring; a small sample of excavated sites in Africa is the best explanation for any perceived 'flickering' pattern."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Integration of these latest data with the findings from the Human Genome Project suggest that human foragers in subsaharan Africa developed modern cognition and behaviour by at least 50,000 BP, before the estimated timing of adaptive radiation out of Africa during the last glacial period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-82">[82]</a></sup> The development of behavioural modernity and sophisticated hunting and foraging methods in subsaharan Africa is thought to date from at least 50,000 years ago. This may have been due to an earlier climatic change to much drier and colder conditions during the last ice age, between 135,000 and 75,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> This was what led to human groups who were seeking refuge from the inland droughts, expanded along the coastal marshes rich in shellfish and other resources. Since sea levels were low due to so much water tied up in glaciers, such marshlands would have occurred all along the southern coasts of Eurasia. The use of rafts and boats may well have facilitated exploration of offshore islands and travel along the coast, and eventually permitted expansion to New Guinea and then to Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, there was also expansion up all major rivers in between, and also around the eastern coastline of Eurasia. As glaciers receded, AMH moved deeper into the Eurasian interior. It now is also suggested that there may have been interbreeding with other subspecies of <i>Homo</i> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denisovans" title="Denisovans">Denisovans</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> whenever these were encountered.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-20th-_and_early_20th-century_publications">Pre-20th- and early 20th-century publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pre-20th- and early 20th-century publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Descent_of_Man" title="The Descent of Man">The Descent of Man</a></i>: <i>And Selection in Relation to Sex</i>, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=LYEQAAAAYAAJ" rel="nofollow">Volume 1</a>; <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Na9LAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow">Volume 2</a>. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_publications">Contemporary publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomically_modern_human&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Contemporary publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

Saunders. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=vNi68aZp874C" rel="nofollow">680 pages</a></li>
<li><i>The Origins of Modern Humans: Biology Reconsidered</i>. By <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130614075946/http://soa.illinoisstate.edu/faculty_staff/faculty.aspx?control=facultyProfile&amp;ID=fsmith&amp;dept=Sociology%20and%20Anthropology" rel="nofollow">Fred H. <li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=5rJvxisKlSAC" rel="nofollow"><i>Becoming Human</i></a>: <i>Evolution and Human Uniqueness</i>. By <a class="external text" href="http://www.amnh.org/science/divisions/anthro/bio.php?scientist=tattersall" rel="nofollow">Ian Tattersall</a>.</li>

By <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=rR9XPnaqvCMC" rel="nofollow">673 pages</a>.</li>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Humans and<br/>
